<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_White_Rock.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/KFC_White_Rock.jpg/220px-KFC_White_Rock.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/KFC_White_Rock.jpg/330px-KFC_White_Rock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/KFC_White_Rock.jpg/440px-KFC_White_Rock.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2712" data-file-height="1525" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_White_Rock.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A KFC restaurant in <a href="/wiki/White_Rock,_British_Columbia" title="White Rock, British Columbia">White Rock, British Columbia</a>, Canada</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span></h4>
<p>The first KFC opened in 1955<span class="noprint">&#59;&#32;64&#160;years ago</span><span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">1955</span>)</span> in <a href="/wiki/Saskatoon" title="Saskatoon">Saskatoon</a>, Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> In 1957, Colonel Harland Sanders gave an interview to <a href="/wiki/CBC_Radio" title="CBC Radio">CBC Radio</a><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> detailing his experience with Canadian food that led to the opening of one of his restaurants in Canada.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, KFC is known as "PFK", due to <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_the_French_Language#Commerce_and_business" title="Charter of the French Language">provisions of the provincial government's Charter of the French Language</a> that restrict use of English on signs and in business names.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kfc_taco_bell.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Kfc_taco_bell.jpg/220px-Kfc_taco_bell.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Kfc_taco_bell.jpg/330px-Kfc_taco_bell.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Kfc_taco_bell.jpg/440px-Kfc_taco_bell.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1216" data-file-height="912" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kfc_taco_bell.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A co-branded KFC-Taco Bell in <a href="/wiki/Oscoda,_Michigan" title="Oscoda, Michigan">Oscoda, Michigan</a></div></div></div>
<p>KFC sales in the United States in 2013 were estimated at $4.22 billion by Technomic.<sup id="cite_ref-fila_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fila-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The basic model for KFC in the United States, not necessarily duplicated elsewhere, is a focus on low prices, a limited menu (29 items on average) and an emphasis on takeout.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Fried_chicken#Racial_stereotype" title="Fried chicken">"very strong percentage" of sales</a> come from African American customers.<sup id="cite_ref-africanamerican_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-africanamerican-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Many KFC locations are <a href="/wiki/Colocation_(business)" title="Colocation (business)">co-located</a> with either <a href="/wiki/Taco_Bell" title="Taco Bell">Taco Bell</a> or Pizza Hut, or other Yum! restaurants.<sup id="cite_ref-Cartwright2003_55-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cartwright2003-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> When Yum! owned <a href="/wiki/Long_John_Silver%27s" title="Long John Silver&#39;s">Long John Silver's</a> and <a href="/wiki/A%26W_Restaurants" title="A&amp;W Restaurants">A&amp;W Restaurants</a>, these brands were often co-branded with KFC as well.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> Often these locations behave like a single restaurant, offering one menu with food items from both restaurant brands.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoFE-33_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoFE-33-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> In 2003, there were 354 KFC-Taco Bell combines, offering the full KFC menu and Taco Bell items, and 13 units offering the full KFC menu and a limited number of Pizza Hut items.<sup id="cite_ref-Cartwright2003_55-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cartwright2003-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The concept originated in 1991, when a KFC-Taco Bell combination opened in <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Darden1_26-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Darden1-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Some locations were also opened as combinations of KFC, Taco Bell and Pizza Hut, but this failed to catch on, and Yum! CEO David Novak blamed a lack of franchisee commitment for its lack of success.<sup id="cite_ref-Novak2012_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Novak2012-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Initially, Sanders and KFC used <a href="/wiki/Hydrogenated_vegetable_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogenated vegetable oil">hydrogenated vegetable oil</a> for frying, but in the 1980s the company began to switch to cheaper oils such as <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm</a> or <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">soybean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated19_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated19-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> In the 2000s it became apparent that these oils contain relatively high levels of <a href="/wiki/Trans_fat" title="Trans fat">trans fat</a>, which increases the risk of <a href="/wiki/Heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>. By April 2007, the chain had switched to trans fat-free soybean oil in all of its US outlets.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2008, Novak credited low US sales as being the result of a lack of new ideas and menu items.<sup id="cite_ref-courierlow_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-courierlow-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> The Spring 2009 launch of Kentucky Grilled Chicken only resulted in a temporary halt to the sales decline.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated23_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated23-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> In 2010 KFC announced a turnaround plan that included improving restaurant operations, introducing <a href="/wiki/Value_theory" title="Value theory">value</a> items and providing healthier menu options.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated23_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated23-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> In the same year, <i><a href="/wiki/Advertising_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Advertising Age">Advertising Age</a></i> noted that KFC was losing market share to its smaller chicken restaurant rival, <a href="/wiki/Chick-fil-A" title="Chick-fil-A">Chick-fil-A</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> In 2011 <a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_News" title="Bloomberg News">Bloomberg News</a> referred to KFC US as "an also-ran to McDonald's Corp".<sup id="cite_ref-AutoFE-36_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoFE-36-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> magazine described how many of the KFC outlets were "aged and uninviting", and that the chain "hasn't introduced an exciting new food item in ages".<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>KFC was described in 2012 by <i><a href="/wiki/Bloomberg_Businessweek" title="Bloomberg Businessweek">Bloomberg Businessweek</a></i> as a "muscular player" in developing regions, specifically Africa, China and India, while noting its falling market share in the US to rivals such as Chick-fil-A and <a href="/wiki/Popeyes" title="Popeyes">Popeyes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated21_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated21-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Some analysts speculated that KFC would begin spinning off its ailing US operations.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated21_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated21-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> That year, the company began divesting control of company-owned US restaurants to franchised operations, with the intention of reducing overall company ownership from 35 percent to 5 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated21_159-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated21-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mexico">Mexico</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/KFC_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg/220px-KFC_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/KFC_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg/330px-KFC_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/KFC_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg/440px-KFC_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg 2x" data-file-width="836" data-file-height="596" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Oaxaca_Mexico.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A KFC restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca,_Oaxaca" class="mw-redirect" title="Oaxaca, Oaxaca">Oaxaca city, Mexico</a></div></div></div>
<p>KFC was the first fast food restaurant opened in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>, in 1963 in <a href="/wiki/Monterrey" title="Monterrey">Monterrey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated72_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated72-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> By 2017, there were 341 outlets in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated73_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated73-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceania">Oceania</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Derwent_Park_Hobart_Australia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/KFC_Derwent_Park_Hobart_Australia.jpg/220px-KFC_Derwent_Park_Hobart_Australia.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/KFC_Derwent_Park_Hobart_Australia.jpg/330px-KFC_Derwent_Park_Hobart_Australia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/KFC_Derwent_Park_Hobart_Australia.jpg/440px-KFC_Derwent_Park_Hobart_Australia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Derwent_Park_Hobart_Australia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A KFC outlet in <a href="/wiki/Derwent_Park,_Tasmania" title="Derwent Park, Tasmania">Derwent Park</a>, a northern suburb of <a href="/wiki/Hobart" title="Hobart">Hobart</a>, Australia.</div></div></div>
<p>There are over 600 KFC outlets in Australia, and around 100 in New Zealand.<sup id="cite_ref-yumstores_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yumstores-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> KFC was the first American style fast food chain to open in both countries.<sup id="cite_ref-newzealandjournal_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newzealandjournal-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> In 2013, KFC reported an annual turnover of almost 2 billion AUD for its Australia and New Zealand operations.<sup id="cite_ref-ceomag_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceomag-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span></h4>
<p>Yum! directly operates 160 KFC outlets in Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-ceomag_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceomag-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The largest of the 53 independent franchisees in Australia is <a href="/wiki/Collins_Foods" title="Collins Foods">Collins Foods</a>, which operates 169 stores.<sup id="cite_ref-ceomag_163-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceomag-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-collinsbuy_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collinsbuy-164">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> KFC's major poultry suppliers in Australia are <a href="/wiki/Inghams_Enterprises" title="Inghams Enterprises">Inghams</a>, Steggles and Turi Foods.<sup id="cite_ref-ceomag_163-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceomag-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The first Australian KFC was opened in 1968 in <a href="/wiki/Guildford,_New_South_Wales" title="Guildford, New South Wales">Guildford</a>, a suburb of <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dixon2002_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dixon2002-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> The franchise was owned by a Canadian entrepreneur called Bob Lapointe.<sup id="cite_ref-Jensen2011_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen2011-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Between 1970 and 1971, 75 outlets were opened.<sup id="cite_ref-Jensen2011_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen2011-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> This had a major impact on Australian chicken production, which increased by 38 percent during the period.<sup id="cite_ref-Jensen2011_166-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jensen2011-166">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> By 1995 there were 452 outlets, and the company employed 12,000 staff.<sup id="cite_ref-Dixon2002_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dixon2002-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> That year, Australia produced 35 percent of KFC's international earnings.<sup id="cite_ref-Dixon2002_165-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dixon2002-165">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Zealand">New Zealand</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Auckland_CBD.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/KFC_Auckland_CBD.jpg/220px-KFC_Auckland_CBD.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/KFC_Auckland_CBD.jpg/330px-KFC_Auckland_CBD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/KFC_Auckland_CBD.jpg/440px-KFC_Auckland_CBD.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4128" data-file-height="3096" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Auckland_CBD.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A KFC restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Auckland_CBD" title="Auckland CBD">Auckland CBD</a>, New Zealand</div></div></div>
<p>The first KFC opened in New Zealand in 1971 at <a href="/wiki/Royal_Oak,_New_Zealand" title="Royal Oak, New Zealand">Royal Oak</a>, a suburb of <a href="/wiki/Auckland" title="Auckland">Auckland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kfcau_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kfcau-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> By 1980 there were 37 outlets.<sup id="cite_ref-newzealandjournal_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newzealandjournal-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> In 1989, PepsiCo acquired the 50 percent stake in KFC New Zealand that it did not already own from the local <a href="/wiki/Goodman_Fielder" title="Goodman Fielder">Goodman Fielder</a> conglomerate.<sup id="cite_ref-kfcau_167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kfcau-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> In 1991 New Zealand turnover topped 100 million NZD for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-kfcau_167-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kfcau-167">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Products">Products</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Shrimp_Burger_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/KFC_Shrimp_Burger_2007.jpg/220px-KFC_Shrimp_Burger_2007.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/KFC_Shrimp_Burger_2007.jpg/330px-KFC_Shrimp_Burger_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/KFC_Shrimp_Burger_2007.jpg/440px-KFC_Shrimp_Burger_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Shrimp_Burger_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>KFC shrimp burger in KFC Vietnam, 2007</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:16-03-02-Hot-Wings-KFC-Berlin-N3S_3711.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/16-03-02-Hot-Wings-KFC-Berlin-N3S_3711.jpg/220px-16-03-02-Hot-Wings-KFC-Berlin-N3S_3711.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/16-03-02-Hot-Wings-KFC-Berlin-N3S_3711.jpg/330px-16-03-02-Hot-Wings-KFC-Berlin-N3S_3711.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/16-03-02-Hot-Wings-KFC-Berlin-N3S_3711.jpg/440px-16-03-02-Hot-Wings-KFC-Berlin-N3S_3711.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4048" data-file-height="2848" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:16-03-02-Hot-Wings-KFC-Berlin-N3S_3711.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hot Wings menu set in KFC Berlin</div></div></div>
<p>KFC's core product offering is <a href="/wiki/Pressure_frying" title="Pressure frying">pressure fried</a>, on-the-bone chicken pieces seasoned with Colonel Harland Sanders' "Original Recipe" of 11 herbs and spices.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> The product is typically available in either two or three piece individual servings, or in a family size cardboard bucket, typically holding between 6 and 16 chicken pieces. In territories that follow the system handed down by Colonel Sanders, such as Canada and the UK, each chicken is divided into nine different <a href="/wiki/Poultry#Cuts_of_poultry" title="Poultry">cuts</a> (2 drumsticks, 2 thighs, 2 wings, 2 breast pieces and one <a href="/wiki/Keel_(bird_anatomy)" title="Keel (bird anatomy)">keel</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> however the United States now uses an eight piece cut.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The product is hand-breaded at individual KFC outlets with <a href="/wiki/Wheat_flour" title="Wheat flour">wheat flour</a> mixed with seasoning in a two- to four-minute process.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakrishnan132_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakrishnan132-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ceomag_163-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceomag-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> It is then pressure fried for between seven and ten minutes (the timing differs between countries) in oil at 185 degrees <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">Celsius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakrishnan132_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakrishnan132-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-cooktemp_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cooktemp-172">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-howitsmade_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-howitsmade-173">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Following this, the chicken is left to stand for 5 minutes in order for it to sufficiently cool before it is placed in the warming oven.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakrishnan132_57-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakrishnan132-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> It is KFC policy to discard chicken if it has not been sold within 90 minutes, in order to ensure freshness.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakrishnan132_57-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakrishnan132-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The frying oil varies regionally, and versions used include <a href="/wiki/Sunflower_oil" title="Sunflower oil">sunflower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soybean_oil" title="Soybean oil">soybean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rapeseed" title="Rapeseed">rapeseed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-steyn_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-steyn-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> A KFC executive stated that the taste of the chicken will vary between regions depending on the oil variety used, and whether the chicken has been corn-fed or wheat-fed.<sup id="cite_ref-steyn_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-steyn-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As well as its core chicken on the bone offering, KFC's major products include chicken sandwiches (including the Zinger and the Tower); <a href="/wiki/Wrap_(sandwich)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wrap (sandwich)">wraps</a> ("Twisters" and "Boxmasters"); and a variety of <a href="/wiki/Finger_food" title="Finger food">finger foods</a>, including crispy chicken strips and <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_wing" title="Buffalo wing">hot wings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Popcorn_Chicken" class="mw-redirect" title="Popcorn Chicken">Popcorn Chicken</a> is one of the most widely available KFC products, and consists of small pieces of fried chicken.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated24_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated24-176">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> In some locations, such as in Australia and Malaysia, <a href="/wiki/Chicken_nugget" title="Chicken nugget">chicken nuggets</a> are also sold.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Zinger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/KFC_Zinger.jpg/220px-KFC_Zinger.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/KFC_Zinger.jpg/330px-KFC_Zinger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/KFC_Zinger.jpg/440px-KFC_Zinger.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Zinger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>KFC's Zinger served with crispy <a href="/wiki/Fries" class="mw-redirect" title="Fries">fries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sweet_chili_sauce" title="Sweet chili sauce">Thai chili sauce</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shah_Alam" title="Shah Alam">Shah Alam</a>, Malaysia. (January 2019)</div></div></div>
<p>KFC adapts its menu internationally to suit regional tastes, and there are over three hundred KFC menu items worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated5_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated5-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> Some locations, such as the UK and the US, sell <a href="/wiki/Grilled" class="mw-redirect" title="Grilled">grilled</a> chicken.<sup id="cite_ref-brazer_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brazer-178">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-grilled_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grilled-179">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> In predominantly <a href="/wiki/Islamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic">Islamic</a> countries, the chicken served is <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Balakrishnan132_57-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Balakrishnan132-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In Asia there is a preference for spicy foods, such as the Zinger chicken sandwich.<sup id="cite_ref-AutoFE-29_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoFE-29-180">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> Some locations in the US sell fried chicken <a href="/wiki/Liver_(food)" title="Liver (food)">livers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gizzard#In_cuisine" title="Gizzard">gizzards</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> A small number of US outlets offer an <a href="/wiki/All-you-can-eat" class="mw-redirect" title="All-you-can-eat">all-you-can-eat</a> <a href="/wiki/Buffet" title="Buffet">buffet</a> option with a limited menu.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of territories, such as Japan, Jamaica, Trinidad, Barbados, Ecuador and Singapore sell fried <a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a> products under the "Colonel's Catch" banner.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> In Jamaica, what was originally a seasonal offering for the <a href="/wiki/Lent" title="Lent">Lent</a> period was expanded to a year-round offering from 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-lent_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lent-184">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Value_menu" title="Value menu">Value menu</a> items are sold under the "Streetwise" name in locations such as Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-streetwise_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-streetwise-185">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Side_dish" title="Side dish">Side dishes</a> often include <a href="/wiki/French_fries" title="French fries">French fries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coleslaw" title="Coleslaw">coleslaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbecue" title="Barbecue">barbecue</a> <a href="/wiki/Baked_beans" title="Baked beans">baked beans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corn_on_the_cob" title="Corn on the cob">corn on the cob</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mashed_potato" title="Mashed potato">mashed potato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bread_roll" title="Bread roll">bread rolls</a> and American <a href="/wiki/Biscuit_(bread)" title="Biscuit (bread)">biscuits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> Salads include the <a href="/wiki/Bean_salad" title="Bean salad">bean salad</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caesar_salad" title="Caesar salad">Caesar salad</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Garden_salad" class="mw-redirect" title="Garden salad">garden salad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> In a number of territories, KFC sell <a href="/wiki/Onion_ring" title="Onion ring">onion rings</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> In Asia, rice based side dishes such as congee are often sold.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated12_72-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated12-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In the US and Greece, <a href="/wiki/Potato_wedges" title="Potato wedges">potato wedges</a> are sold instead of French fries.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/McCormick_%26_Company" title="McCormick &amp; Company">McCormick &amp; Company</a> is KFC's largest supplier of sauces, seasonings and marinades, and is a long-term partner in new product development.<sup id="cite_ref-ceomag_163-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ceomag-163">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the company's previous relationship with PepsiCo, most territories supply PepsiCo products, but exceptional territories include Barbados, Greece, New Zealand, the Philippines, Romania, South Africa, and Turkey, which stock drinks supplied by <a href="/wiki/The_Coca-Cola_Company" title="The Coca-Cola Company">The Coca-Cola Company</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aruba" title="Aruba">Aruba</a>, which stocks <a href="/wiki/RC_Cola" title="RC Cola">RC Cola</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Cott_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cott Corporation">Cott Corporation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-romaniadrinks_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-romaniadrinks-192">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> In Peru, the locally popular <a href="/wiki/Inca_Kola" title="Inca Kola">Inca Kola</a> is sold.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> In a number of Eastern European locations and Portugal, <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> is offered, in addition to soft drinks.<sup id="cite_ref-russiabeer_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-russiabeer-196">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-port_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-port-198">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Launched in 2009, the Krusher/Krushem range of frozen beverages containing "real bits" such as <a href="/wiki/Kit_Kat" title="Kit Kat">Kit Kat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oreo" title="Oreo">Oreo</a> and strawberry <a href="/wiki/Shortcake" title="Shortcake">shortcake</a>, is available in over 2,000 outlets.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> Egg <a href="/wiki/Custard_tart" title="Custard tart">custard tart</a> is a popular dessert worldwide, but other items include <a href="/wiki/Ice_cream_sundae" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice cream sundae">ice cream sundaes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tres_leches_cake" title="Tres leches cake">tres leches cake</a> in Peru.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, the "KFC am" breakfast menu began to be rolled out internationally, including such items as <a href="/wiki/Pancake" title="Pancake">pancakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Waffle" title="Waffle">waffles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porridge" title="Porridge">porridge</a>, as well as fried chicken.<sup id="cite_ref-jargon_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jargon-201">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On August 27, 2019, KFC tested meatless boneless wings and chicken nuggets in Atlanta, Georgia.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="11_herbs_and_spices">11 herbs and spices</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/KFC_Original_Recipe" title="KFC Original Recipe">KFC Original Recipe</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Original_Recipe_chicken_in_bucket.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/KFC_Original_Recipe_chicken_in_bucket.jpg/220px-KFC_Original_Recipe_chicken_in_bucket.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/KFC_Original_Recipe_chicken_in_bucket.jpg/330px-KFC_Original_Recipe_chicken_in_bucket.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/KFC_Original_Recipe_chicken_in_bucket.jpg/440px-KFC_Original_Recipe_chicken_in_bucket.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KFC_Original_Recipe_chicken_in_bucket.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>KFC Original Recipe fried chicken</div></div></div>
<p>Sanders' Original Recipe of "11 herbs and spices" is one of the most famous <a href="/wiki/Trade_secret" title="Trade secret">trade secrets</a> in the catering industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Chartrand_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chartrand-205">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The recipe is not <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patented</a>, because patent law requires public disclosure of an invention and provides protection only for a strictly limited term, whereas trade secrets can remain the <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_property" title="Intellectual property">intellectual property</a> of their holders in perpetuity.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A copy of the recipe, signed by Sanders, is held inside a safe inside a vault in KFC's Louisville headquarters, along with eleven vials containing the herbs and spices.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated21_159-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated21-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> To maintain the secrecy of the recipe, half of it is produced by Griffith Laboratories before it is given to <a href="/wiki/McCormick_%26_Company" title="McCormick &amp; Company">McCormick</a>, who add the second half.<sup id="cite_ref-crossan_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crossan-209">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1999, a couple who bought the house formerly occupied by Colonel Sanders found scribbled notes purported to be the secret recipe. Initially KFC wanted to file a lawsuit against the couple to stop an auction of the notes, but by early 2001, it dropped the lawsuit, claiming the scribbled notes are "nowhere close" to the original recipe.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Joe Ledington of Kentucky, a nephew by marriage of Colonel Sanders, claimed to have found a copy of the original KFC fried chicken recipe on a handwritten piece of paper in an envelope in a scrapbook. In August 2016, <i><a href="/wiki/Chicago_Tribune" title="Chicago Tribune">Chicago Tribune</a></i> staffers conducted a cooking test of this recipe and claimed after a few attempts that, with the addition of <a href="/wiki/Monosodium_glutamate" title="Monosodium glutamate">MSG</a> flavor-enhancer, <a href="/wiki/Ac%27cent" class="mw-redirect" title="Ac&#39;cent">Ac'cent</a>, they produced fried chicken which tasted "indistinguishable" from the chicken they purchased at KFC.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment">Equipment</span></h3>
<p>KFC initially used stove-top covered <a href="/wiki/Cooking_pot" class="mw-redirect" title="Cooking pot">cooking pots</a> to fry its chicken.<sup id="cite_ref-shelton_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shelton-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> In the 1960s, the officially recommended model was the L S Hartzog developed "KFC 20-Head Cooker", a large device that cost $16,000.<sup id="cite_ref-winston_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-winston-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> The Hartzog model had no oil filtration system, meaning that filtering had to be done manually, and the pressure fryers occasionally exploded, often causing harm to employees.<sup id="cite_ref-shelton_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shelton-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> In 1969, inventor and engineer <a href="/wiki/Winston_L._Shelton" title="Winston L. Shelton">Winston L. Shelton</a> developed the "Collectramatic" pressurized fryer to overcome the problems KFC faced in quickly frying chicken to meet growing customer demand. The Collectramatic used precision time and temperature controls and self-filtered the cooking oil – all while meeting Colonel Sanders' legendary high quality standards.<sup id="cite_ref-shelton_212-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shelton-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Fred Jeffries, then vice president of purchasing at KFC, claimed that the invention helped fuel the company's rapid expansion and success:
</p>
<blockquote><p>There's no way it (KFC) could have grown like it did without the Collectramatic. Stores were doing about $200,000 a year in sales on average with the pots ... but they could never have done the $900,000 a year it became without Win's fryer. He (Shelton) helped set the stage for that with true engineering thinking.<sup id="cite_ref-shelton_212-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shelton-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Although a number of franchisees bought the Collectramatic, which had the support of Colonel Sanders from 1970 onwards, John Y. Brown had given tacit approval to franchisees to exclusively use the older L S Hartzog fryer, saying "Though those old pots were damn dangerous, at least we knew they worked! I was mostly afraid these new fryers would break down in the middle of business."<sup id="cite_ref-shelton_212-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shelton-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Brown warned franchisees that they were in violation of their contract if they used the Collectramatic.<sup id="cite_ref-shelton_212-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shelton-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> Brown held his ground on the issue until he learned that his father, <a href="/wiki/John_Y._Brown_Sr." title="John Y. Brown Sr.">John Y. Brown Sr.</a>, who owned multiple KFC franchises, was successfully using the Collectramatic in every franchise he owned.<sup id="cite_ref-shelton_212-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shelton-212">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>  The issue was eventually resolved after Heublein purchased KFC, acquired Hartzog and nullified the contract.<sup id="cite_ref-winston_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-winston-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> The Collectramatic has been an approved pressure fryer for KFC from 1972 onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-winston_213-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-winston-213">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 2013 onwards, KFC has been transitioning from using Collectramatic cookers to pressure fryers produced by <a href="/wiki/Henny_Penny_(manufacturer)" title="Henny Penny (manufacturer)">Henny Penny</a>, which supplies KFC with various equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> The 'Velocity' series of pressure fryers includes increased load capacity, automatic oil filtration and increased oil longevity.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Advertising">Advertising</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/KFC_advertising" title="KFC advertising">KFC advertising</a></div>
<p>Colonel Sanders was a key component of KFC advertising until his death in 1980. Despite his death, Sanders remains a key icon of the company as an "international symbol of hospitality".<sup id="cite_ref-harvardbiz_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harvardbiz-215">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Early official slogans for the company included "North America's Hospitality Dish" (from 1956) and "We fix Sunday dinner seven nights a week".<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> The "finger lickin' good" slogan was used from 1956, and went on to become one of the best-known slogans of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated6_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated6-218">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup> The trademark expired in the US in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> The first KFC logo was introduced in 1952 and featured a "Kentucky Fried Chicken" typeface and a logo of the Colonel.<sup id="cite_ref-logohist_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-logohist-220">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Advertising played a key role at KFC after it was sold by Sanders, and the company began to advertise on US television with a budget of US$4 million in 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-rood_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rood-221">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> In order to fund nationwide advertising campaigns, the Kentucky Fried Chicken Advertising Co-Op was established, giving franchisees ten votes and the company three when deciding on budgets and campaigns. In 1969, KFC hired its first national advertising agency, <a href="/wiki/Leo_Burnett" title="Leo Burnett">Leo Burnett</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Darden1_26-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Darden1-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> A notable Burnett campaign in 1972 was the "Get a bucket of chicken, have a barrel of fun" <a href="/wiki/Jingle" title="Jingle">jingle</a>, performed by <a href="/wiki/Barry_Manilow" title="Barry Manilow">Barry Manilow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Darden1_26-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Darden1-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> By 1976 KFC was one of the largest advertisers in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-Georgescu2005_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Georgescu2005-222">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies_and_criticism">Controversies and criticism</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_Fried_Cruelty" class="mw-redirect" title="Kentucky Fried Cruelty">Kentucky Fried Cruelty</a></div>
<p>Since the beginning of the 21st century, fast food has been criticized for its <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a> record, its links to <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> and its environmental impact.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eric_Schlosser" title="Eric Schlosser">Eric Schlosser</a>'s book <i><a href="/wiki/Fast_Food_Nation" title="Fast Food Nation">Fast Food Nation</a></i> (2002) and <a href="/wiki/Morgan_Spurlock" title="Morgan Spurlock">Morgan Spurlock</a>'s film <i><a href="/wiki/Super_Size_Me" title="Super Size Me">Super Size Me</a></i> (2004) reflected these concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated26_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated26-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Since 2003, <a href="/wiki/PETA" class="mw-redirect" title="PETA">People for the Ethical Treatment of Animals (PETA)</a> has protested KFC's choice of poultry suppliers worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> The exception is KFC Canada, which signed an agreement pledging to only use "animal friendly" suppliers.<sup id="cite_ref-WilliamsChampion2011_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WilliamsChampion2011-225">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> President of KFC's US division Gregg Dedrick said PETA mischaracterized KFC as a poultry producer rather than a purchaser of chickens.<sup id="cite_ref-Swann2010_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Swann2010-226">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, Yum! stated: "[As] a major purchaser of food products, [Yum!] has the opportunity and responsibility to influence the way animals supplied to us are treated. We take that responsibility very seriously, and we are monitoring our suppliers on an ongoing basis."<sup id="cite_ref-an08_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-an08-227">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, <a href="/wiki/Greenpeace" title="Greenpeace">Greenpeace</a> accused KFC Europe of sourcing the <a href="/wiki/Soya_bean" class="mw-redirect" title="Soya bean">soya bean</a> for its chicken feed from <a href="/wiki/Criticisms_of_Cargill#Controversy_around_Santarém_port_and_Amazon_deforestation" title="Criticisms of Cargill">Cargill</a>, which had been accused of clearing large swathes of the <a href="/wiki/Amazon_rainforest" title="Amazon rainforest">Amazon rainforest</a> in order to grow the crop.<sup id="cite_ref-cargill_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cargill-228">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2010,  according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>, "in the US where fried chicken remains closely associated with age-old racist stereotypes about black people in the once segregated south", KFC Australia aired a thirty-second promotion on television named "KFC's cricket survival guide" which shows a <a href="/wiki/Cricket" title="Cricket">Cricket</a> fan surrounded by fans from the opposing team. The television announcer asks, "Need a tip when you're stuck in an awkward situation?" the fan passes around his "bucket of KFC", even though the commercial was intended for a group of Australian modern artists that protested against abstract expressionism, the commercial found its way to social media in the United States, prompting sharp disapproval. KFC Australia made a statement to the fact the commercial was "misinterpreted by a segment of people in the US" and it was a "light-hearted reference to the West Indian cricket team" and "The ad was reproduced online in the US without KFC's permission, where we are told a culturally-based stereotype exists, leading to the incorrect assertion of racism... We unequivocally condemn discrimination of any type and have a proud history as one of the world's leading employers for diversity".<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2012, Greenpeace accused KFC of sourcing <a href="/wiki/Pulp_(paper)" title="Pulp (paper)">paper pulp</a> for its food packaging from <a href="/wiki/Deforestation_in_Indonesia" title="Deforestation in Indonesia">Indonesian</a> rainforest wood.<sup id="cite_ref-rainforest_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rainforest-230">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Independent forensic tests showed that some packaging contained more than 50 percent mixed tropical hardwood fiber, sourced from <a href="/wiki/Asia_Pulp_%26_Paper" title="Asia Pulp &amp; Paper">Asia Pulp &amp; Paper</a> (APP).<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated13_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated13-231">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AutoFE-25_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AutoFE-25-232">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> APP said such fiber can be found in recycled paper, or: "It can also come from tree residues that are cleared, after a forest area has become degraded, logged-over or burned, as part of a sustainable development plan. APP has strict policies and practices in place to ensure that only residues from legal plantation development on degraded or logged-over forest areas and sustainable wood fiber enters the production supply chain."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated13_231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated13-231">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> KFC said: "From a global perspective, 60 percent of the paper products that Yum! (our parent company) sources are from sustainable sources. Our suppliers are working towards making it 100 percent."<sup id="cite_ref-rainforest_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rainforest-230">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Boycott_KFC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Boycott_KFC.jpg/220px-Boycott_KFC.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Boycott_KFC.jpg/330px-Boycott_KFC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Boycott_KFC.jpg/440px-Boycott_KFC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Boycott_KFC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Protesters demonstrating outside a KFC restaurant in <a href="/wiki/Royal_Oak,_Michigan" title="Royal Oak, Michigan">Royal Oak, Michigan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In December 2012, the chain was criticized in China when it was discovered that a number of KFC suppliers had been using <a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">growth hormones</a> and an excessive amount of <a href="/wiki/Antibiotics" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibiotics">antibiotics</a> on its poultry in ways that violated Chinese law.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated36_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated36-233">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> In February 2013, Yum! CEO David Novak admitted that the scandal had been "longer lasting and more impactful than we ever imagined."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated36_233-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated36-233">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> The issue is of major concern to Yum!, which earns almost half of its profits from China, largely through the KFC brand. In March 2013, Yum! reported that sales had rebounded in February, but that lower sales in December and January would result in a decline in same-store sales of 20 percent in the first quarter.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, KFC was fined £950,000 after two workers were scalded by boiling hot gravy. The company admitted to charges of failing in a duty of care to employees, and was ordered by Teesside Crown Court to pay fines of £800,000 and £150,000.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2018, logistics mismanagement by <a href="/wiki/DHL" title="DHL">DHL</a>, which had been selected by KFC UK as their new delivery partner, caused a chicken shortage in the United Kingdom – KFC's largest market in Europe – forcing the company to temporarily close hundreds of restaurants around the country.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> KFC apologized by taking out adverts in British newspapers with the company's initials scrambled to read "FCK" on it followed by an apology, which was well received.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
